        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer373479055" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9695204849732612395">This is a book that I don’t think I would have read if it weren’t for Goodreads. I probably would never have even heard of it. Technically, I suppose this obscure novel would be considered “historical fiction,” but that’s misleading. It is that, but it is also biography, philosophy, meditation, poetry.<br/><br/>Hadrian was Emperor of Rome from AD 117 to 138. Marguerite Yourcenar wrote this novel in the form of a memoir, written by Hadrian near the end of his life and addressed to then 17-year old future e</span>
  <span id="freeText9695204849732612395" style="display:none">This is a book that I don’t think I would have read if it weren’t for Goodreads. I probably would never have even heard of it. Technically, I suppose this obscure novel would be considered “historical fiction,” but that’s misleading. It is that, but it is also biography, philosophy, meditation, poetry.<br><br>Hadrian was Emperor of Rome from AD 117 to 138. Marguerite Yourcenar wrote this novel in the form of a memoir, written by Hadrian near the end of his life and addressed to then 17-year old future emperor Marcus Aurelius. Hadrian discusses his public role and his attempts to use diplomacy more than bloodshed. By the standards of the Roman Empire, his reign was considered peaceful (this in spite of a war with the Jews resulting in hundreds of thousands of deaths and the banishment of the Jewish people from Jerusalem). <br><br>Most of the emperor’s recollections, however, focus on the personal, even the trivial. He reflects on moderation in diet, his love of hunting, and his admiration for Greek culture. With a reserved tone that belies his depth of feeling, he relates his love for the young Bithynian, Antinous, and his sorrow at Antinous’ death. Hadrian deals with his grief by deifying the youth and creating a cult which long outlasts them both. There must be hundreds of statues of Antinous in the world’s museums today, a testament to an emperor’s attempt to cope with a very personal sorrow.<br><br><a href="http://www.flickr.com/photos/mg2001/9167762890/" title="Antinous by medg2001, on Flickr" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380384765i/743385.jpg" width="358" height="500" alt="Antinous" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><i>Antinous</i><br><br>Yourcenar seems to channel this character of antiquity, speaking with an authentic dignity and distance that is not modern in feel. Hadrian speaks to us, but not in a tell-all confessional. In her notes on the writing of the novel, Yourcenar quotes from Flaubert about the period when Hadrian lived, “Just when the gods had ceased to be, and the Christ had not yet come, there was a unique moment in history, between Cicero and Marcus Aurelius, when man stood alone.” Her Hadrian is a man of that age, not ours.<br><br>The mood of this book is quiet, thoughtful, and peaceful. It evokes the feeling of walking among ancient ruins, with the eerie sensation that comes when other tourists are out of view, as the warm Italian sun gleams off fragments of stone, and for a moment there is a strange perception that the ruins are whole again, with time somehow distorted.<br><br><a href="http://www.flickr.com/photos/mg2001/9165542147/" title="HadrianVilla by medg2001, on Flickr" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380384765i/743386.jpg" width="375" height="500" alt="HadrianVilla" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><i>Hadrian's Villa, Tivoli</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="9695204849732612395" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/54697-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1353271036p2/54697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/15219211?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/54697-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=fictions-of-the-big-it">fictions-of-the-big-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=worldly-lit">worldly-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=underrated-lost-classics">underrated-lost-classics</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer15219211" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8066022338006565844">Gorgeously written, wise and stately. Meditative, deep in a philosophical probing sort of way, moves smoothly and contains a sort of magnificence...the prose is given room to breathe. I have pretty much every reason to believe it's not taking too many liberties with historical accuracy. Yourcenar spent years researching it and getting the details right and it shows. <br/><br/>Her notes on the research and composition at the end are illuminating and tersely eloquent...worth the price of admission in their </span>
  <span id="freeText8066022338006565844" style="display:none">Gorgeously written, wise and stately. Meditative, deep in a philosophical probing sort of way, moves smoothly and contains a sort of magnificence...the prose is given room to breathe. I have pretty much every reason to believe it's not taking too many liberties with historical accuracy. Yourcenar spent years researching it and getting the details right and it shows. <br /><br />Her notes on the research and composition at the end are illuminating and tersely eloquent...worth the price of admission in their own respect.<br /><br />It's virtually unknown...a diamond in the rough. I would quote it at length but I just don't have the copy handy. Highly, insistently, awestruck recommendation for this one....<br /><br />this is the kind of book which one wants to speak about at great length but I sense that its power is also the type which absorbs perhaps too deeply for simple summary....<br /><br />Read it and continue to recommend it to your friends. There is much wisdom here, philosophical speculation, psychological insight, historical grandeur and subtle, eloquent, illuminating prose. <br /><br />Plus, there's some etchings of various Roman sites of antiquity thrown in which are rather breathtaking even when viewed in black and white on the page. <br /><br />Seriously, if you're reading this....READ THIS GODDAMN BOOK!<br /><br />***<br /><br />I thought it would be worth mentioning that I recently picked this book up again by chance (it was given back to me from a friend who'd read it and put it on his bookshelf for maybe 6 months...isn't it funny how sometimes books you borrow end up sort of absorbing into your own collection, adopted unconsciously into the family of your library and vice versa? Do books have a gravitational pull for their rightful owners?) and read it for solace in the evening after the bottom dropped out in a rather important part of my life. It soothed me, it moved me, the stoic (lower case s) wisdom of the man was calming and enlightening. Cleared the mind while enchanting the imagination, like a subtle wine on a summer afternoon. <br /></span>

<div id="review_3251208071" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3251208071" />
    <a title="Jan-Maat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat"><img alt="Jan-Maat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407579968p2/5617661.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3251208071?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan-Maat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan-Maat" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat">Jan-Maat</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140181944" href="/book/show/1040679.Memoirs_Of_Hadrian">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=read-in-translation">read-in-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3251208071" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9961028882527683013">No, this is not an uplifting book. Except that it is, rather as poor Atlas uplifts the Heavens.<br/><br/>At first I admired this book much more than I liked it, until I reached the central chapters which crystallised it for me.<br/><br/>Yourcenar has written a book about a lonely, depressed, ageing, Roman Emperor Hadrian suffering from a congenital heart condition, and if that does not sound enticing enough to the prospective reader, he has been brooding over a certain event which he perceives, or he shows us uncon</span>
  <span id="freeText9961028882527683013" style="display:none">No, this is not an uplifting book. Except that it is, rather as poor Atlas uplifts the Heavens.<br /><br />At first I admired this book much more than I liked it, until I reached the central chapters which crystallised it for me.<br /><br />Yourcenar has written a book about a lonely, depressed, ageing, Roman Emperor Hadrian suffering from a congenital heart condition, and if that does not sound enticing enough to the prospective reader, he has been brooding over a certain event which he perceives, or he shows us unconsciously, as illuminating his entire life. <br /><br />The book is written in the form of a letter to his adopted successor the future Emperor Marcus Aurelius <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ who you may be familiar with from such films as <a href="https://www.imdb.com/title/tt0058085/" rel="nofollow noopener"><i>The Fall of the Roman Empire<i></i></i></a> and <a href="https://www.imdb.com/title/tt0172495/" rel="nofollow noopener"><i> Gladiator</i></a>  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. I am not entirely convinced that the Hadrian we meet in this book would have written this intimate letter to his designated successor, we don't see him being intimate in that way at all in the book - which no doubt is part of his problem.<br /><br />Yourcenar struggled with writing the book - she started shortly after Robert Graves wrote <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18765.I__Claudius__Claudius___1_" title="I, Claudius (Claudius, #1) by Robert Graves" rel="nofollow noopener"> I, Claudius</a> in the 1930s (which may have been coincidental or something to do with the Zeitgeist, she does not mention that book as an inspiration) and she had several false starts, struggling to find a way of letting Hadrian speak without being drowned out by other voices. The form of a letter seems a perfect solution, it is effectively a monologue but without the idea that he is silencing other voices <br /><br />At one point in the narrative Hadrian thinks of himself as a god, or at the very least god's instrument on earth; <i>"I perceived differently my relations with the divine. I could see myself as seconding the deity in his effort to give form and order to a world, to develop and multiply its convolutions, extensions, and complexities. I was one of the segments of the wheel, an aspect of that unique force caught up in the multiplicity of things; I was eagle and bull, man and swan, phallus and brain all together, a Proteus who is also a Jupiter.<br />And it was at about this time that I began to feel myself divine."</i> (p.127), and at a certain point further on in the narrative this suddenly seemed very reasonable as I vaguely recalled the story of Zeus and Semele, as Yourcenar essentially tells much the same tale, the god desires to love, but a mortal drawn too close to a god will be burnt up. The mentality of the Greek and Roman gods was such that they could cope with that kind of thing <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ burnt up mortal <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, or at least they could cope with it better than Yourcenar's Hadrian does.<br /><br />Yourcenar's technique was to read, or rather steep herself in the writings of the Roman empire during the second century, there is not much surviving about Hadrian directly <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ and less than she thought that is reliable  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> so this involved reading round him, this she felt enabled her to inhabit, as far as possible, the same mental world as Hadrian. she says that she was inspired by a letter of Flaubert's; <i> "In turning the pages of a volume of Flaubert's correspondence much read and heavily underscored by me about the year 1927 I came again upon this admirable sentence:<br />'Just when the gods had ceased to be, and the Christ had not yet come, there was a unique moment in history, between Cicero and Marcus Aurelius, when man stood alone.' A great part of my life was going to be spent in trying to define, and then to portray, that man existing alone and yet closely bound with all being."</i> (p. 269).<br /><br />Hmm, just as the Simpsons <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ hmm, two simpsons references in one review, that's probably a record <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ and not a good one <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> were still praying for the great depression to end, so too for me unbaptised and certainly not pagan, the human stands alone. The great thing about this novel for me, is how Yourcenar was both inspired by Flaubert yet in her fiction transcends his statement. We see how Hadrian is initiated into Mithraism and the Eleusinian Mysteries and how their notions of life emerging from death intersect with his personal experience.<br /><br />And this is a tremendous literary work - it is all about the intersections, everything is interwoven, this is the kind of book of which I can believe that Yourcenar sweated over adding a comma in the right place in the morning and in the afternoon realised that it had to be taken out again.<br /><br />But appallingly anglo-centric as I am, Yourcenar's book led me not to think of Flaubert but of Gibbon's judgement in his <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19400.The_Decline_and_Fall_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="The Decline and Fall of the Roman Empire by Edward Gibbon" rel="nofollow noopener">the Decline and fall of the Roman empire</a> that there was a happy era of human existence under the 'good' emperors around the time of Hadrian. Maybe this was an unconscious attraction for her, inspired in the inter-war period and completing her book after the Second World War she is writing about a world leader who drew back from conflict and war and presided, aside from a major Jewish revolt, over a period of peace and certainty, the age of the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pantheon,_Rome#/media/File:Giovanni_Paolo_Panini_-_Interior_of_the_Pantheon,_Rome_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" rel="nofollow noopener">Pantheon</a> when religious cults co-existed, apparently calmly, with philosophical speculation, it is not like <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ or does not seem to be <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> the 1930s and 40s in Europe, it is a safe haven.<br /><br />Yourcenar's Hadrian's thoughts suggest a feeling that time is cyclical <i>"this firmament will become again what it was in Hipparchus' time; it will be again what it is in the time of Hadrian"</i> (p.130) in the context of the novel that might have a purely personal meaning to Hadrian, but we can also read it more broadly - the era of violence and disorder will pass, order and peace will arise from it<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ but only temporarily - lets have none of that optimism here! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />I recalled also Mary Renault who, like Yourcenar, lived in a same sex relationship and set some of her fiction in a past when certain kinds of same sex relationships were culturally sanctioned. Perhaps that fed into the appeal that Hadrian had to Yourcenar. Yourcenar's not-wife, Grace Frick, translated the book into English - a literal labour of love.<br /><br />If for me this is a four star novel then Yourcenar's notes on her writing process which complete the volume are five star an utter delight, but maybe I think this is better than a four star novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9961028882527683013" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Sidharth Vardhan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58296635-sidharth-vardhan"><img alt="Sidharth Vardhan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595750367p2/58296635.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1721707738?book_show_action=true">Apr 02, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sidharth Vardhan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sidharth Vardhan" href="/user/show/58296635-sidharth-vardhan">Sidharth Vardhan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1721707738" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13370981008929153599"><blockquote>
  <i><br/>“But books lie, even those that are most sincere.”<br/></i>
</blockquote><br/>It is supposed to be historically most accurate novel - I can’t judge about that but I’m willing to take the word of knowledgeable people on that. What is so far more incredible is the way the author managed to make herself invisible in her work – you know how novels have their authors’ personality in them. You can’t normally come out of a novel without having some idea of author’s personality. Narrators of Proust and Celine look like so much</span>
  <span id="freeText13370981008929153599" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i><br>“But books lie, even those that are most sincere.”<br></i>
</blockquote><br>It is supposed to be historically most accurate novel - I can’t judge about that but I’m willing to take the word of knowledgeable people on that. What is so far more incredible is the way the author managed to make herself invisible in her work – you know how novels have their authors’ personality in them. You can’t normally come out of a novel without having some idea of author’s personality. Narrators of Proust and Celine look like so much like their mirror images; in other cases it is true to a lesser extent – but not in this case. The only thing you will have guessed about Yourcenar by reading MoH, is that she is genius.<br><br>If I believed in spirits, I could have asserted that Hadrian’s had possessed Yourcenear. An innocent reader can easily led to believe that is written by someone who if not a king, is a really old man living in ancient Rome.<br><br>The narrative is first person – so we enter with a bit of suspicion about the reliability but soon that suspicion is removed. Hadrian is old and looking forward to his inevitable death. I guess different people react differently at that stage – Hadrian has grown a bit distant from his own self – distant enough to look at his own self objectively: <br><blockquote>
  <i><br>“I have come to speak of myself, at times, in the past tense.”<br></i>
</blockquote><br>Another thing which shadow of death does is that it makes king of one of most powerful empires look so much like an ordinary, powerless man.<br><br>Not that Hadrian is your regular arrogant kings. Besides the hard qualities of builders, soldiers and generals that you would expect from a Roman king; he has the soft qualities of being knowledgeable, philosophical, lover of arts, at times poetical and perhaps wise; which we associate with people of ancient Greece – and Goodreads. His philosophical reflections and lyrical prose is almost seductive. <br><br>Rowling once said, "To a well organised mind, death is but the next best adventure." That is Yourcenar 's Hadrian for you. The narrator impresses on reader’s mind an image of wise old man accepting his inevitable death with a confidence of conviction (rather than arrogance of ignorance as is normally the case) and giving his last lesson (in most lyrical language) to his disciple; perhaps with one hand raised to heavens like in ‘Death of Socrates’.<br><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1486874527i/21957339._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>I’ve whole pages of quotes from the book. The following are only a small sample:<br><i><br>“Morals are matter of private agreement; decency is of public concern.”<br><br>“We talk much of the dreams of youth. Too often we forget its scheming.” <br><br>“To me, who had not yet given first place to anything except to ideas or projects, or at the most to a future image of myself, this simple devotion of man to man seemed prodigious and unfathomable. No one is worthy of it, and I am still unable to account for it.”<br><br>“I knew that good like bad becomes a routine, that the temporary tends to endure, that what is external permeates to the inside, and that the mask, given time, comes to be the face itself.”<br><br>“I am not sure that the discovery of love is necessarily more exquisite than the discovery of poetry.”</i></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="13370981008929153599" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1988567081" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1988567081" />
    <a title="Margitte" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7481492-margitte"><img alt="Margitte" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1361772481p2/7481492.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1988567081?book_show_action=true">May 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Margitte" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Margitte" href="/user/show/7481492-margitte">Margitte</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140087642" href="/book/show/953431.Memoirs_of_Hadrian">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=2017-read">2017-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=biographical-fiction">biographical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=biography-and-memoir">biography-and-memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1988567081" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6820082364495023702">The statue of Hadrian, the 14th Emperor of the Roman Empire, was brought alive by the French author Marguerite Yourcenar in this novel. She climbed into his thoughts, philosophies and personality and wrote his memoir for him. Hadrian was never a conqueror, but rather a strong leader who brought controversial changes to the Roman laws which made life more bearable and humane for the vast empire.<br/><br/>By allowing Hadrian to be the protagonist of his own letter to Marcus Aurelius, the long forgotten man </span>
  <span id="freeText6820082364495023702" style="display:none">The statue of Hadrian, the 14th Emperor of the Roman Empire, was brought alive by the French author Marguerite Yourcenar in this novel. She climbed into his thoughts, philosophies and personality and wrote his memoir for him. Hadrian was never a conqueror, but rather a strong leader who brought controversial changes to the Roman laws which made life more bearable and humane for the vast empire.<br /><br />By allowing Hadrian to be the protagonist of his own letter to Marcus Aurelius, the long forgotten man was recalled from the dead, his life and history revived. Like an archaeologist, the author uncovered the relics from the past that was buried deep in the mind of an emperor who thought differently about humanity, leadership and statesmanship.<br /><br />Although this novel was published in 1951, it was already finished in the 1940s and became an instant success. Her hope was that Churchill could become the same kind of leader as the humane Hadrian was and bring peace to the world. <br /><br />Hadrian was Spanish by birth, Roman by descend, Greek by culture, and a peacemaker by principle.<br /><br />The epistolary-style novel deserves the accolades it received. It is a piece of linguistic art. Philosophical and introspective in style. The translation was brilliant.<br /><br />An interesting article on Hadrian the emperor can be viewed here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.theguardian.com/books/2008/jul/19/history">https://www.theguardian.com/books/200...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="6820082364495023702" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_9491091" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/9491091" />
    <a title="Jessica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/419287-jessica"><img alt="Jessica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1289534648p2/419287.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/9491091?book_show_action=true">Nov 24, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica" href="/user/show/419287-jessica">Jessica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780374503482" href="/book/show/1100618.Memoirs_of_Hadrian">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">friends; romans; countrymen</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jessica by: </span>
      paul, ginnie, david g.
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=leetle-boys">leetle-boys</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=love-and-other-indoor-sports">love-and-other-indoor-sports</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=wish-i-owned">wish-i-owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=groups-of-people">groups-of-people</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=happyendings">happyendings</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer9491091" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4565948847375824918">This book is not nearly as funny as the similarly titled <i>Diaries of Adrian Mole,</i> so don't get them confused! In fact, this book is not funny at all, which is probably my only serious criticism of it. Other than that, it is pretty fucking great.<br/><br/>Um yeah, so it kind of makes my brain hurt that someone wrote this book. I'll probably write a real review soon, it being so good and all.... In the meantime though -- and in case I die suddenly or see something shiny and get distracted, and don't get arou</span>
  <span id="freeText4565948847375824918" style="display:none">This book is not nearly as funny as the similarly titled <i>Diaries of Adrian Mole,</i> so don't get them confused! In fact, this book is not funny at all, which is probably my only serious criticism of it. Other than that, it is pretty fucking great.<br /><br />Um yeah, so it kind of makes my brain hurt that someone wrote this book. I'll probably write a real review soon, it being so good and all.... In the meantime though -- and in case I die suddenly or see something shiny and get distracted, and don't get around to it -- I must note that I think the somewhat creepy, suspicious hype surrounding this book is well deserved, and then some. You know that one pair of pants you have that makes it look like you've got a terrific ass, even though in reality, most days, you might not in truth? This book was a bit like that, except instead of flattering your butt, reading it makes you feel smarter than you probably are. Not annoying smart, either -- or I'd at least like to think so -- but just more thoughtful and interested in abstract ideas and whatnot than you actually might be in normal life. It did take me awhile to get truly absorbed, but all the "work" did pay off, and I really recommend it. A reader who, unlike me, knows anything AT ALL about the Roman Empire and what have you would get more out of this than I did and would feel up to speed. I myself am fairly ignorant of the classical world, and what affection I've got tends to be for Greece. Fortunately for us, though, Hadrian felt the same (about Greece being more appealing, that is; he was up on his Rome).<br /><br />Yeah and so, this a fabulous novel which really explores some fascinating territory and the potential of that form, and of our brains and humanity and mortality and whatnot. Human experience, blah blah blah blah.... History and something something, blah blah blah blah. It's really good! I just can't be articulate about it, mostly because I'm embarrassed even to try. I would not recommend this to people who find that everything about the Roman Empire leaves them cold; for everyone else, though, I'd say give it a shot. For me this book was the level of "hard" where I found it hard to concentrate on reading while other people were talking. It was the level of "good," though, where I'd tell them to shut up, or at least I'd get up and walk to the other end of the subway car where it was quieter. This book changed my thoughts, which is kind of all I want. I didn't just think about what was happening in the narrative -- just to clarify, nothing was happening; it was essentially <i>Gilead</i>, if you've read that, only instead of a dying Iowa preacher with heart trouble writing a letter to a young boy, it was a dying Roman emperor with heart trouble writing a letter to a young man -- I thought about the world and civilization and the experience of being a human being differently.... which, you know, I appreciate. I mean, I can use that. Who couldn't?<br /><br />At the end of this volume, in Yourcenar's "Reflections on the Composition of Memoirs of Hadrian," (yeah, it's that kind of book) she quotes this line from Flaubert's letters: "Just when the gods had ceased to be, and the Christ had not yet come, there was a unique moment in history, between Cicero and Marcus Aurelius, when man stood alone." The book is about that moment. It's about a lot of other stuff too, though. It's got what I think is one of the most unique and memorable literary love stories. And pictures! It's got beautiful pictures. And it's just excruciatingly well-written..... During the first quarter of reading this, I noticed that I was getting really depressed about my life and lack of accomplishment and just feeling like a total loser all the time, and then I realized why: I was comparing myself to the Roman Emperor Hadrian! Compared to Hadrian, I really am a big loser. I mean a BIG loser. But it's not a fair comparison. I was talking last weekend to this somewhat patrician gentleman (I use the term "gentleman" loosely) about this book, and he told me that they read this as undergraduates at Harvard, where according to him many readers suffer from the opposite problem.<br /><br />Anyway, I'm rambling on, and I don't mean to. It's past my bedtime, and I can't say anything worthwhile about this book, so I'm just sort of yammering away uselessly. Where I think I might have been going with that I'm-no-Roman-emperor line was: Yourcenar's project has an inherent empathy problem, which she solves. I'll never be the most powerful man in the world, and I won't even ever be the erudite and brilliant Marguerite Yourcenar, who was, the back cover informs us, "the first woman to be elected to the prestigious French Academy" and who, the cover further notes, in an intimidatingly sober tone, "writes only in French." But that's okay. I've got a library card. Apparently, as I'm learning, that gets me close enough.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4565948847375824918" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_83951580" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/83951580" />
    <a title="Eric" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26852-eric"><img alt="Eric" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1383287017p2/26852.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/83951580?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eric" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eric" href="/user/show/26852-eric">Eric</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780374503482" href="/book/show/1100618.Memoirs_of_Hadrian">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26852-eric?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26852-eric?shelf=historiophantasmagoria">historiophantasmagoria</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer83951580" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11725394010125741738"><i>What are masterpieces? Let us name a few...the Testament of Villon, the Essays of Montaigne, the Fables of La Fontaine, the Maxims of La Rochefoucald and La Bruyère, the Fleurs du Mal and Intimate Journals of Baudelaire...In feeling, these masterpieces contain the maximum of emotion compatible with a classical sense of form. Observe how they are written; many are short and compressed, fruits of reflective and contemplative natures, prose or poetry of great formal beauty and economy of phrase. Th</i></span>
  <span id="freeText11725394010125741738" style="display:none"><i>What are masterpieces? Let us name a few...the Testament of Villon, the Essays of Montaigne, the Fables of La Fontaine, the Maxims of La Rochefoucald and La Bruyère, the Fleurs du Mal and Intimate Journals of Baudelaire...In feeling, these masterpieces contain the maximum of emotion compatible with a classical sense of form. Observe how they are written; many are short and compressed, fruits of reflective and contemplative natures, prose or poetry of great formal beauty and economy of phrase. There are no novels, plays or biographies included on the list, and the poetry is of the kind that speculates on life. They have been chosen by one who most values the art which is distilled and crystallized out of a lucid, curious and passionate imagination. All these writers enjoy something in common, “jusqu’au sombre plaisir d’un coeur mélancolique”: a sense of perfection and a faith in human dignity, combined with a tragic apprehending of the human situation, and its nearness to the Abyss.</i> <br /><br /><br />Add <i>Memoirs of Hadrian</i> to Cyril Connolly’s Latinate, festal-funereal list, on which it will manifest the novel (where’s <i>Madame Bovary</i>?), and perhaps biography, a mode Yourcenar at once avoids and transcendently augments. Like <i>Lolita</i>, <i>Memoirs of Hadrian</i> is a model of the sustained and elaborate testamentary <i>récit</i>, the world told in a dying voice. Warrior-peacemaker, austere sensualist, skeptic/cultist, philosopher/mythomane, avid connoisseur of all gods and rites, of humanity’s myriad senses of the sacred, the mobile and various emperor Hadrian, “that man alone and yet closely bound with all being,” is an ideal humanist mouthpiece, Yourcenar his learned animator and prefect of “that imperial guard which poets and humanists mount in relay around any great memory”:<br /><br /><blockquote>I have sometimes thought of constructing a theory of human knowledge which would be based on eroticism, a theory of contact wherein the mysterious value of each being is to offer us just that point of perspective which another world affords. In such a philosophy pleasure would be a more complete but also more specialized approach to the Other, one more technique for getting to know what is not ourselves.</blockquote> <br /><br />Hadrian wants to know where flesh becomes soul, how the experienced body becomes the soul’s knowledge and transport:<br /><br /><blockquote>Thus from each art practiced in its time I derive a knowledge which compensates me in part for pleasures lost. I have supposed, and in my better moments think so still, that it would be possible in this manner to participate in the existence of everyone; such sympathy would be one of the least revocable kinds of immortality. There have been moments when that comprehension tried to go beyond human experience, passing from the swimmer to the wave. But in such a realm, since there is nothing exact left to guide me, I verge upon the world of dream and metamorphosis.</blockquote><br /><br />Reading <i>the swimmer to the wave</i>, and another passage in which Hadrian pictures the body’s sleep as a time when the soul is washed back out into an unconscious ocean of all being, I was reminded of similar imagery in a novel I read a few weeks ago, <i>A Single Man</i>, whose author Christopher Isherwood and protagonist George Falconer were, like Hadrian, aging lovers of boyish partners, though only George and Hadrian were deprived of Antinous. I took down Isherwood’s <i>Diaries</i> and in 1955, “I read Marguerite Yourcenar’s novel about Hadrian, which bored me at the time, but which I now feel has penetrated quite deeply into my consciousness at some other level.” Both novelists explore the consciousness of the dying animal; and read again, George’s drunken Forsterian Only Connect outburst shows its affinity with Hadrian’s faith in sensual receptivity and earthy rootedness as passageways to other states. Borges once wrote that the powerful recurrence of human dreams is greater miracle than any of the biblical levitations or apparitions. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11725394010125741738" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_481791012" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/481791012" />
    <a title="Warwick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53165-warwick"><img alt="Warwick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510406793p2/53165.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/481791012?book_show_action=true">Dec 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Warwick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Warwick" href="/user/show/53165-warwick">Warwick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9782070369218" href="/book/show/1112273.M_moires_d_Hadrien">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=rome">rome</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=ancient-rome">ancient-rome</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer481791012" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9023032212879563690">Near the beginning of this book, in one of its many lyrical and precise descriptive passages, Hadrian writes about his intimations of mortality.<br/><br/><blockquote><i>Comme le voyageur qui navigue entre les îles de l'Archipel voit la buée lumineuse se lever vers le soir, et découvre peu à peu la ligne du rivage, je commence à apercevoir le profil de ma mort.</i><br/><br/>[As the traveller navigating between the islands of the Archipelago sees the luminous mist rise towards the evening, and discovers, little by little, the line of t</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText9023032212879563690" style="display:none">Near the beginning of this book, in one of its many lyrical and precise descriptive passages, Hadrian writes about his intimations of mortality.<br /><br /><blockquote><i>Comme le voyageur qui navigue entre les îles de l'Archipel voit la buée lumineuse se lever vers le soir, et découvre peu à peu la ligne du rivage, je commence à apercevoir le profil de ma mort.</i><br /><br />[As the traveller navigating between the islands of the Archipelago sees the luminous mist rise towards the evening, and discovers, little by little, the line of the shore, so I begin to notice the contours of my death.]</blockquote><br /><br />This passage sets out perfectly both the book's theme – mortality – and its method - a melancholy prose style whose brilliance can sometimes take your breath away.<br /><br />I was hugely impressed by <i>Mémoires d'Hadrien</i>. Purporting to be the memoirs of the Roman emperor, Yourcenar's book pulls off the narrative voice so well that you sometimes have to remind yourself that it's fiction; every sentence seems heavy with the wise sadness of someone who has lived for a long time and through many momentous events.<br /><br />The novel took more than twenty years to write and the quality shows in every line, every phrase. It's not a perfect book, perhaps – although it's short, it is dense (like the book Alice's sister was reading, it contains no pictures or conversations), and I found it dragged slightly in the back end – but that's admittedly perhaps because I was reading it in French.<br /><br />Though the book is a life story, it is also tightly-controlled. This is not a sprawling epic, but rather a thematic portrait of a man at the end of his life dwelling mostly on those experiences which have come to preoccupy him, primarily his own impending death and the moments of love which – just perhaps – will have made it all worthwhile.<br /><br />For Hadrian, in Yourcenar's conception of him, love and death are closely intertwined. Perhaps that is why he can't leave either of them alone. Architecture sets him off: his passages on the immortality of buildings represent a great meditation on architecture to be set beside that of Hugo in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1253933.Notre_Dame_de_Paris" title="Notre-Dame de Paris by Victor Hugo" rel="nofollow noopener">Notre-Dame de Paris</a>.<br /><br /><blockquote><i>Ces murs que j'étaie sont encore chauds du contact de corps disparus; des mains qui n'existent pas encore caresseront ces fûts de colonnes.</i><br /><br />[These walls that I prop up are still warm from contact with bodies that have disappeared; hands which do not yet exist will caress the trunks of these columns.]</blockquote><br /><br />Ideas of death being transcended through architecture are followed by sketches of deaths variously from old age, natural disaster, war, and suicide. Hadrian does not draw conclusions from this catalogue of mortality, but the reader is well able to if he or she wishes. There is also an interesting section where Hadrian reflects on his own deification: as emperor, the people consider him literally to be a god, something which, characteristically, he tries to find useful:<br /><br /><blockquote><i>Loin de voir dans ces marques d'adoration un danger de folie ou de prépotence pour l'homme qui les accepte, j'y découvrais un frein, l'obligation de se dessiner d'après quelque modèle éternel, d'associer à la puissance humaine une part de suprème sapience. Être dieu oblige en somme à plus de vertus qu'être empereur.</i><br /><br />[Far from seeing in these signs of adoration a risk of madness or authoritarianism for the man who accepts them, I found them to be a restraint – the obligation to model oneself on some eternal prototype, to link human power to an element of supreme wisdom. Being a god, in short, calls for more virtues than being an emperor.]</blockquote><br /><br />Part of the impetus for the novel, Yourcenar has said, was a fascination with this period of history when belief in the Olympian gods had disappeared but before Christianity had really emerged – a brief moment, in Flaubert's phrase, when man alone existed. This book evokes the idea perfectly.<br /><br />There is a looming sense of disaster in all this brooding on death, a disaster which finally comes with the fate of Hadrian's beloved Antinous. There is something exceptionally artful in the way that Antinous's story takes up only a small part of the novel, while the ramifications are yet so infused in every sentence Hadrian writes. Yourcenar – or Hadrian – is coy about the physical side of their relationship, but the book is full of brilliant and perceptive comments on love as an emotion.<br /><br /><blockquote><i>Mais le poids de l'amour, comme celui d'un bras tendrement posé au travers d'une poitrine, devenait peu à peu lourd à porter.</i><br /><br />[But the weight of love, like an arm draped tenderly across one's chest, became little by little heavy to bear.]</blockquote><br /><br />It's in this character of Antinous that the themes of death and love are united – and the reason, perhaps, that they are so united in Hadrian's mind. It's a union that means Hadrian is reluctant to ignore death or pass over its unsavoury features: he's determined to consider it as fully as he can, and understand what he himself is facing.<br /><br /><blockquote><i>Cette mort serait vaine si je n'avais pas le courage de la regarder en face, de m'attacher à ces réalités du froid, du silence, du sang coagulé, des membres inertes, que l'homme recouvre si vite de terre [...].</i><br /><br />[This death would be in vain if I did not have the courage to look at it head-on, to concentrate on these realities of cold, of silence, of coagulated blood, of inert limbs, that man recovers so quickly from the earth.]</blockquote><br /><br />In a way the whole book is an attempt to do this, only somehow it's not half as depressing as I just made it sound. On the contrary, it's life-affirming, moving and thought-provoking – and built from a prose style which, on occasion, looks something like genius.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9023032212879563690" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2101081648" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2101081648" />
    <a title="Elie F" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/54680202-elie-f"><img alt="Elie F" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1575940654p2/54680202.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2101081648?book_show_action=true">Jun 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elie F" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elie F" href="/user/show/54680202-elie-f">Elie F</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780374503482" href="/book/show/1100618.Memoirs_of_Hadrian">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54680202-elie-f?shelf=french">french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54680202-elie-f?shelf=female-writer">female-writer</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54680202-elie-f?shelf=greek-roman">greek-roman</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2101081648" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13140187631668206016"><b>"I was beginning to find it natural, if not just, that we should perish."</b><br/><br/>This memoir is an old man's efforts to bear with humanity a bit longer and make his looming death easier. Contemplating on his brief life and all of its random yet heavy choices, he finds it impossible to bring all the contradictions in his life into accord, and yet he tries to find peace in death without too much obsessive concern with people and things yet unborn. What eases death for him the most is the rememberance of t</span>
  <span id="freeText13140187631668206016" style="display:none"><b>"I was beginning to find it natural, if not just, that we should perish."</b><br /><br />This memoir is an old man's efforts to bear with humanity a bit longer and make his looming death easier. Contemplating on his brief life and all of its random yet heavy choices, he finds it impossible to bring all the contradictions in his life into accord, and yet he tries to find peace in death without too much obsessive concern with people and things yet unborn. What eases death for him the most is the rememberance of the death of the beloved Antinous who had <i>"served to enrich but also to simplify my life."</i> Those who enrich and simplify our lives happen to be the ones who enrich and simplify our death as well; the memory of them seem to inform us that we no longer belong to this world, and only death can reconnect us with those to whom we belong. Hadrian/Yourcenar thinks of Homer: <i>And the shade of Patroclus appears at Achilles' side.</i> The isle of Achilles, also the isle of Patroclus, has become a secret abode for him: <i>"I shall doubtless be there at the moment of my death."</i> Hadrian vaguely applies this conclusion drawn from the meditation on his own death to the impending demise of Roman/Greek civilization as well. At a certain time, a civilization will demand no more refinement or success or life, because it finds itself already belonging more with the dead than with the living.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13140187631668206016" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_886587095" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/886587095" />
    <a title="Jasmine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17309926-jasmine"><img alt="Jasmine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571294643p2/17309926.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/886587095?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jasmine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jasmine" href="/user/show/17309926-jasmine">Jasmine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141184968" href="/book/show/1040676.Memoirs_of_Hadrian">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17309926-jasmine?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17309926-jasmine?shelf=en-fran%C3%A7ais">en-français</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17309926-jasmine?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17309926-jasmine?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17309926-jasmine?shelf=modern-classics">modern-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17309926-jasmine?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17309926-jasmine?shelf=penguin-modern-classics">penguin-modern-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer886587095" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer157128110077898530"><b>"Just when the gods had ceased to be, and the Christ had not yet come, there was a unique moment in history, between Cicero and Marcus Aurelius, when man stood alone.” (Gustave Flaubert).</b><br/><br/>Gustave Flaubert’s quote is to some extent the catalyst for Marguerite Yourcenar’s relationship with Hadrian, the Roman emperor who lived from 76 AD to 138 AD – a man she comes to know better than her own father: <i>‘The facts of my father’s life are less known to me than those of the life of Hadrian.’</i> (quotes in i</span>
  <span id="freeText157128110077898530" style="display:none"><b>"Just when the gods had ceased to be, and the Christ had not yet come, there was a unique moment in history, between Cicero and Marcus Aurelius, when man stood alone.” (Gustave Flaubert).</b><br /><br />Gustave Flaubert’s quote is to some extent the catalyst for Marguerite Yourcenar’s relationship with Hadrian, the Roman emperor who lived from 76 AD to 138 AD – a man she comes to know better than her own father: <i>‘The facts of my father’s life are less known to me than those of the life of Hadrian.’</i> (quotes in italic from 'Reflection on the Composition'). She first has the idea of writing a book about Hadrian when she is in her twenties, but after several attempts realizes that she is too young: <i>'There are books which one should not attempt before having passed the age of forty…..It took me years to learn how to calculate exactly the distances between the emperor and myself.’</i> She then burns some of the notes that she has taken. <br /><br />But Hadrian does not let go of her. He pops up here and there over the following years. In an artist’s shop, she discovers a Piranesi engraving named ‘View of Hadrian’s Villa’. She writes an essay on Greek mythology where Hadrian’s name appears. She continues reading authors from classical antiquity and realizes that <i>'…one of the best ways to reconstruct a man’s thinking is to rebuild his library.’</i> Eventually, in 1948, she discovers some four or five typewritten sheets with the salutation ‘My dear Mark…’. Finally, she realizes that Mark stands for Marcus Aurelius and that she has a fragment of her manuscript in her hands, which she had believed to be lost. From this point on, she takes up her work on Hadrian again. With some meticulous research, she is able to bring to life an emperor about whom, unfortunately, so little is known. Scholarship is not sufficient for the creation of this fascinating portrait, however: <i>'One foot in scholarship, the other in magic arts, or more accurately and without metaphor, absorption in that sympathetic magic which operates when one transports oneself, in thought, into another’s body and soul.’</i>. And she knows that she has to take herself out of the picture: <i>‘Keep one’s own shadow out of the picture; leave the mirror clean of the mist of one’s own breath; take only what is most essential and durable in us….’</i>. The result of all those years of research, reflection, meditation and ultimately writing is ‘Mémoires d’Hadrien’ – a book which, I can state here without hesitation, is very special. <br /><br />‘Memoirs of Hadrian’ consists of a valedictory letter that Hadrian writes to his successor, Marcus Aurelius. He reflects on his own life and on life in general, as well as his approaching death. Reading these lines, it is easy to forget that this letter was written by an author who lived in the twentieth century. I had the impression that it must have been written by the dying Roman emperor himself, <i>'a man who was almost wise’</i>. Yourcenar’s writing is so real. Her prose is tranquil, composed and occasionally formal – just as one would expect from a valedictory letter written in Latin. It contains some of the most beautiful and poignant quotes that I have ever read:<br /><br /> <b>'I am not sure that the discovery of love is necessarily more exquisite than the discovery of poetry.’ (p.41)<br /><br />'Our great mistake is to try to exact from each person virtues which he does not possess, and to neglect the cultivation of those which he has' (p.47)<br /><br />'Since hatred, stupidity, and delirium have lasting effects, I saw no reason why good will, clarity of mind and just practice would not have their effects, too.’ (p.90)</b><br /><br />I quote here from the English translation by Grace Frick, done in collaboration with the author. Grace Frick and Marguerite Yourcenar had a romantic relationship, and I am convinced that Frick’s translation grasps the pulchritude of the original. I have read the French version as well as the English translation, and the elegance and beauty of the prose in both is awe-inspiring. <br /><br />It is difficult to describe the emotions that this book evokes in the reader. To understand the philosophical depths of Yourcenar’s writing, and to absorb the beauty of her language, it really must be read. For this reason, I recommend it highly to anyone who likes in-depth reading. You will start asking questions about your own life, your own views, and the way you deal with challenges. Unfortunately, I have the feeling that Yourcenar’s work might not be very well known outside of France. None of my literary friends here (in Zurich) have heard of her, and her books are not stocked in the local bookshops. <br /><br />Even though Yourcenar’s historical research on Hadrian is said to be thorough, I would not classify the book as pure historical fiction. Indeed, readers of historical fiction might be disappointed, as ‘Memoirs of Hadrian’ is rather on the philosophical side. It is more a reflection on life and politics, with no plot to speak of. It is not a fast read; at least, I did not find this to be the case. I would not recommend it as a beach read or an airport novel. Rather, this is a book to be savoured in front of the fire, with a delicious cup of tea or a glass of red wine. In your favourite wing chair, under your favourite tree. Wherever your chosen haunt is – go there with this book, settle down, take your time, and lose yourself in Hadrian’s memoirs. </span>
  <a data-text-id="157128110077898530" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3147991012" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3147991012" />
    <a title="João Barradas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22404289-jo-o-barradas"><img alt="João Barradas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547847493p2/22404289.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3147991012?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="João Barradas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="João Barradas" href="/user/show/22404289-jo-o-barradas">João Barradas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9789896604219" href="/book/show/39348201-mem-rias-de-adriano">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3147991012" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16341516665920268239">A memória é uma das fantásticas funções com que a espécie humana foi aprimorada, sendo ela que, pedra a pedra, edifica o castelo a que chamamos vida. A sua importância ímpar foi reconhecida desde o império helénico que lhe dedicou uma deusa, de nome Mnemosine, mãe das Musas, protectoras das artes. Cedo também se atribuiu ao cérebro, o local de repouso dessas memórias, esse clavário da vida, tão bem protegido por uma calote. Com um estatuto de primor, não é de espantar o medo que reside na perda </span>
  <span id="freeText16341516665920268239" style="display:none">A memória é uma das fantásticas funções com que a espécie humana foi aprimorada, sendo ela que, pedra a pedra, edifica o castelo a que chamamos vida. A sua importância ímpar foi reconhecida desde o império helénico que lhe dedicou uma deusa, de nome Mnemosine, mãe das Musas, protectoras das artes. Cedo também se atribuiu ao cérebro, o local de repouso dessas memórias, esse clavário da vida, tão bem protegido por uma calote. Com um estatuto de primor, não é de espantar o medo que reside na perda destas peças de um puzzle maior, contrabalançado apenas pela alegria de as poder partilhar. E se a noite é a altura do dia em que, num exame mental, revemos os actos praticados; a velhice - a noite da vida - é também ela a idade da reflexão e do saudosismo.<br /><br />Numa longa carta remetida a Marco Aurélio, seu sucessor, Adriano, um dos governantes do império Romano, elenca as diferentes etapas que constituíram a sua existência, no momento da sua agonia final. Relembra a fome insaciável por alcançar um lugar de destaque, talhada por protectores audazes; os conflitos afrodisíacos, plenos de dúvidas e questionamentos, pelos objectos de desejo, sempre relegados a um segundo plano, subconsciente; o sentido de responsabilidade que lhe permitiu manter um próspero império vasto, sem a intervenção de Ares ou Belona; a crítica à mesquinhez de poderes anteriores, fundados em falsos puritanismos e vanglorismo absurdos, mais interessados em servir-se do que em servir um povo; a defesa de valores nobres, patentes na insígnia conhecida “Humanidade, Felicidade, Liberdade”, que se opõem à misoginia, à escravatura, à belicosidade, ao tacanhismo; o amor à Grécia Antiga, modelo de cultura física, mental e espiritual.<br /><br />Repassando estes episódios que construíram uma linha tecida por Moiras inspiradas, a autora oferta um quadro amplo de uma vida repleta das mais belas cores, dos mais instigantes contrastes, de sombras misteriosas, de pinceladas finas, de traços cuidados. Tudo enredado por uma linguagem assertiva e milimétrica, palatável e prazerosa, que requer pausas para reflectir ao sabor das “quebras da terceira folha”, impostas por fluxos de pensamento, e dos factos históricos inseridos com rigor, que requerem consulta acessória. É de surpreender como uma mulher se conseguiu adentrar na mente de um homem, estando eles separados por 18 séculos. Mas as conexões deslindadas, concretizadas num relato em primeira pessoa, criaram pontes que o leitor pode atravessar com segurança e, chegado à outra margem, deslumbrar-se com uma empatia natural pelo protagonista.<br /><br />No término desta longa missiva, o maior post scriptum que poderemos descortinar é a certeza máxima de estarmos perante um testamento vital, pejado de ensinamentos, lições e conselhos, não apenas para moldar um carácter cujo destino será assumir um poder imenso; mas também para nortear outras vidas. Essa intemporalidade torna este livro num clássico perene, a ser folheado por várias gerações vindouras – porque é na análise do passado, que se orienta um presente mais pujante, para um futuro promissor. Que as memórias não nos falhem, que sobrevivam e revivam em outros, para que os erros diminuam, as safras sejam mais fartas e o regozijo se torne imenso, numa prece dirgida a um Zeus mitigado pelo Homem. <br /><br />"Quando se tiver diminuído o mais possível as servidões inúteis, evitado as desgraças desnecessárias, continuará a haver sempre, para manter vivas as virtudes heróicas do homem, a longa série de verdadeiros males, a morte, a velhice, as doenças incuráveis, o amor não correspondido, a amizade recusada ou traída, a mediocridade de uma vida menos vasta que os nossos projectos e mais enevoada que os nossos sonhos: todas as infelicidades causadas pela divina natureza das coisas." (pág. 111)</span>
  <a data-text-id="16341516665920268239" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1036384001" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1036384001" />
    <a title="Carmo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15850971-carmo"><img alt="Carmo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592341202p2/15850971.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1036384001?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carmo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carmo" href="/user/show/15850971-carmo">Carmo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789725686850" href="/book/show/16102279-mem-rias-de-adriano">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15850971-carmo?shelf=b%C3%A9lgica">bélgica</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15850971-carmo?shelf=my-bib">my-bib</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1036384001" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13763232282311352741"><b>Memórias de Adriano</b> é uma ficção histórica em registo epistolar – um género que me é muito caro pela proximidade que estabelece com o leitor. Para quem lê, é como se aquela carta, aqueles segredos, aquela intimidade nos fosse dirigida, a nós em particular.<br/><b>Marguerite Yourcenar</b> planeou e escreveu este livro entre 1924 e 1929. Quando terminou queimou tudo. Tinha na altura 25 anos.<br/>Voltou à obra em 1934 com avanços e recuos até 1939 altura em que o abandona novamente, só recomeçando em 1948. Durante </span>
  <span id="freeText13763232282311352741" style="display:none"><b>Memórias de Adriano</b> é uma ficção histórica em registo epistolar – um género que me é muito caro pela proximidade que estabelece com o leitor. Para quem lê, é como se aquela carta, aqueles segredos, aquela intimidade nos fosse dirigida, a nós em particular.<br /><b>Marguerite Yourcenar</b> planeou e escreveu este livro entre 1924 e 1929. Quando terminou queimou tudo. Tinha na altura 25 anos.<br />Voltou à obra em 1934 com avanços e recuos até 1939 altura em que o abandona novamente, só recomeçando em 1948. Durante os anos de interregno, olho-o de longe com melancolia e desgosto por não se achar capaz de tão grandioso desafio.<br /><br /><i>”Mergulho no desespero de um escritor que não escreve”</i><br /><br />Em Dezembro de 1948 mergulhou com renovado frenesim na vida do Imperador e deu-lhe voz própria.<br /><br /><i>”Retrato de uma voz. Se preferi escrever estas Memórias de Adriano na primeira pessoa foi para dispensar o mais possível qualquer intermediário, ainda que fosse eu própria. <br />Desde aquele momento nada mais me interessou senão rescrever aquele livro, custasse o que custasse.”</i><br /><br />Fazer uma reconstituição desta natureza com tão grande distância temporal, exigiu-lhe inúmeras viagens, pesquisas, comparações, conversas com historiadores, médicos, analistas, um trabalho moroso e extenuante com vista a compilar o maior numero possível de factos, e tentar conciliá-los a todos no final, sem demasiados lapsos.<br /><br />A regra do jogo era: <i>”aprender tudo, ler tudo, informar-se de tudo...”<br />“Fazer o melhor que puder. Refazer. Retocar ainda imperceptívelmente esse retoque.”</i><br /><br />Foram 18 anos de trabalho obsessivo. <b>Yourcenar</b>, adoptou uma forma muito particular de escrita para este livro. Durante a noite ela escrevia e descrevia minuciosamente todas as cenas, todos os cenários criados. Na manhã seguinte queimava tudo e voltava a escrever, agora, só o resumo daquilo que havia feito na noite anterior. <br />Assim, este livro é a concentração dos registos de milhares de páginas, o melhor, a polpa, o néctar, chamem-lhe o que quiserem, é o <i>crème de la crème</i>. E é o que faz com que este livro seja uma proeza apaixonante sobre um dos períodos mais calmos do Império Romano.<br />Quanto ao Imperador, a Adriano, leiam: é um relato de uma vida magnifica, uma reflexão pungente e fascinante. Nada que eu possa dizer fará justiça a esta escrita intensa e sensível, a esta melodia composta com perfeição. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13763232282311352741" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8889115868909508419">Superb roman de reconstrucție istorică, portretul psihologic al împăratului fiind reconstituit după cărțile pe care le avea în bibliotecă. Sau mai bine zis „le-ar fi avut în bibliotecă” deoarece nu s-a păstrat până în zilele noastre colecția de cărți a lui Hadrian.<br/>Dincolo de redarea cu lux de amănunte a vieții în secolul II și a intrigilor politice de la Roma, remarcabil pentru o autoare este modul deosebit de sensibil cum descrie trăirile homosexuale dintre Hadrian și tânărul bithynian Antinous</span>
  <span id="freeText8889115868909508419" style="display:none">Superb roman de reconstrucție istorică, portretul psihologic al împăratului fiind reconstituit după cărțile pe care le avea în bibliotecă. Sau mai bine zis „le-ar fi avut în bibliotecă” deoarece nu s-a păstrat până în zilele noastre colecția de cărți a lui Hadrian.<br />Dincolo de redarea cu lux de amănunte a vieții în secolul II și a intrigilor politice de la Roma, remarcabil pentru o autoare este modul deosebit de sensibil cum descrie trăirile homosexuale dintre Hadrian și tânărul bithynian Antinous. Aspect care probabil a stârnit controversă în 1951 când au fost publicate aceste memorii de ficțiune istorică.<br />O carte deosebit de reușită, iar faptul că traducerea în limba română îi aparține eruditului Mihail Gramatopol sporește valoarea ediției Humanitas.</span>
