<div id="review_494195162" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/494195162" />
    <a title="Aubrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey"><img alt="Aubrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472535045p2/4119460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/494195162?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aubrey" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey">Aubrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/2496418.Memoirs_of_Hadrian">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=french">french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reality-check">reality-check</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=5-star">5-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=prose-prose-prose">prose-prose-prose</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=queer-as-in">queer-as-in</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1-read-on-hand">1-read-on-hand</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=books-are-the-best-invention">books-are-the-best-invention</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-2013">r-2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer494195162" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12228074247605418620">An Ode<br/><br/>Hadrian. Born and bred from seventy-six to one-thirty-eight,<br/>Man, Roman, Emperor from one-seventeen to one-thirty-eight,<br/>Fictionalized in historical form from nineteen-twenty-four to nineteen-fifty-one,<br/>By Woman, French, Writer, from nineteen-o’-three to nineteen-eighty-seven<br/>Near two millennia separate life and chronicle, the event from the research<br/>The Empire caked in so much study, so much praise, so much distortion, <br/>So much misuse, so much inheritance of both thought and form.<br/>You are one of</span>
  <span id="freeText12228074247605418620" style="display:none">An Ode<br /><br />Hadrian. Born and bred from seventy-six to one-thirty-eight,<br />Man, Roman, Emperor from one-seventeen to one-thirty-eight,<br />Fictionalized in historical form from nineteen-twenty-four to nineteen-fifty-one,<br />By Woman, French, Writer, from nineteen-o’-three to nineteen-eighty-seven<br />Near two millennia separate life and chronicle, the event from the research<br />The Empire caked in so much study, so much praise, so much distortion, <br />So much misuse, so much inheritance of both thought and form.<br />You are one of many, Hadrian, chosen here by virtue of the crossroads<br />The intersection of your life with the death of Roman Gods<br />And the struggles of Christianity. Nothing more. Nothing less.<br /><br />Within this theological miasma you have yourself, your youth<br />With its glorious physicality, its flights of poetic fancy, its<br />Slow dissolve into the machinations of power hid behind robes<br />and other panderings at officiated strivings. You came, you saw,<br />you succeeded in the name of Greece and the sense of restraint<br />of one with the broadest stretch of conquered stretch of land<br />The Romans ever did see. All for you to travel upon in your fervency,<br />your desire for aesthetic in all variations available to the landscape.<br />And so you continued on in contented ruling and contented dabbling<br />In realms of indulgence rich in form and thought, spread wide for your perusal.<br /><br />Are you happy, Hadrian? By this clemency, this audacity<br />To reinterpret your mind and all its efforts,<br />Your fate well suited to its Age, where self-awareness<br />Was afforded expression on the widest field at your world’s disposal.<br />An Empire, no less. Upon which the author has seen fit<br />To grant you interests in a multitude of thoughtful meanderings<br />Of Art, Culture, Philosophy, love of the flesh and thrill of the hunt,<br />The Desire for Discovery framed within the Contentment of a Conqueror<br />Connoisseur and Creator on a scale unmatched in these later days<br /> Hemmed in by respect of the Other, the facile flow of Knowledge.<br /><br />I view you, Hadrian, as the flesh views the bones,<br />Acquiescence to the vibrancy of your influence,<br />Contrariness to the limits seemingly imposed<br />By your calcified structures, an inevitability of life<br />That I choose not to fear, but to utilize.<br />Your generosity, your restraint, your insight,<br />Inextricably mixed with your nationalism, your colonialism,<br />The assurance that a wave of your hand would raise a city,<br />Your ostentatious contemplation of your self as a god. <br />You have many followers here, subsumed in your fair and foul.<br /><br />Would it make you angry, Hadrian, if I stripped your sensibilities,<br />Of all its high flown phrases, its prettied up pretenses toward civilization,<br />And simply termed it white savior complex? Remember,<br />I base this on not a lifelong contemplation of your history,<br />But on a fiction, drawn up by a woman no less,<br />One of those you easily consigned to the label of Other<br />Amongst the Jews, the Orientals, the Christians, the Slaves,<br />The Barbarians. Would anything less than a swallowing in entirety<br />Of your triumphs, your feats, your biases that grew in comfort<br />Among the profound insight that I admit to admiring, enrage?<br /><br />You are dead and gone, Hadrian. You were content<br />In viewing your one life, your one culture, as perfection,<br />And were fortunate to find favor with the future,<br />Enough to be addressed eighteen hundred seventy-five years<br />Here, in this casual form, after your death.<br />Where is the rest of your world? Where else does the modern sense<br />Find such material, such scholarship, such vaulted musings,<br />Such ease of access by way of a field long cherished by<br />The ivory towers filled with those that mirror your thoughts,<br />Your self-satisfied ignorance in things beyond your ken.<br /><br />Your proud Rome is split and sundered, and survives<br />In the very forms of insidious servitude your self of fiction despised.<br />The worth of your beloved Greece lies solely in its ancient past,<br />The modern times speak of Homer and leave the actual country<br />To crippling debt and moral ruin. You would cry, I think,<br />And rage, and scream, belying all your talk of peace<br />With self and soul listed in this fictional pages,<br />If you were here to see it. The current Age has no patience<br />For your encapsulated philosophizing, your conquering streak,<br />Your yearning to imprint yourself on the widest stage of remembrance.<br /><br />And yet. I delight in this prose of your life, constrained as it is<br />By the breach of centuries, the warp of fiction, the woof of translation.<br />I find worth in your thoughts, mongering as they are<br />In brutal horrors and easy conscience of a ruler of ages past,<br />The sort of being that, in my world, would reap untold retribution.<br />Never again shall humanity look upon the likes of you, and yet,<br />I find it hard to say good riddance. The road to hell<br />Is paved in well-intentioned displays of power unrestricted<br />By any ramification, any force demanding reconciliation of the soul,<br />And yet, I find it difficult to picture you on such a path.<br /><br />For you loved, once, and found yourself a fool in losing it.<br />For you ruled, once, and found your efforts as so much sand.<br />For you ran, and sprang, in a faithful body that at last betrayed you<br />And sunk your once sensuous musings into a clot of corporal decay.<br />For you lived, once, and strewed great spreads of land with your design,<br />Sought to elevate as you saw fit the ruins of both build and thought. <br />For you soldiered many times, and many times you favored peace.<br />For you died, once, as all humanity does, and made such a life<br />For the histories to laud you evermore as one of the good.<br />And of course, this is a work of fiction. So what do I know.<br /><br />And so, I leave you this ode, a mix of little praise and heavy caution,<br />Grudging admiration and audacious critique. The profile of your death<br />Is not mine to make, not with my veins of cynical forbearance,<br />And lack of interest. You were born, Hadrian, and through fate and fortune<br />Found yourself in a seat of power, made your mark on land and record<br />By all the skills vested by culture and self-interest. And thus the world,<br />Remembers as such, as time melds history with embellishment,<br />And those of the past seep into the newfangled forms of the future.<br />You came, you saw, and now you sleep, to be brought forth<br />In all your good, and all your ill, by minds who see fit to do so.<br /><br />For better or for worse, Hadrian, <br />Publius Aeilus Traianus Hadrianus Augustus,<br />You are known.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12228074247605418620" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_177466259" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/177466259" />
    <a title="Tony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33771-tony"><img alt="Tony" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1292869016p2/33771.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/177466259?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tony" href="/user/show/33771-tony">Tony</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781125891223" href="/book/show/6678990-memoirs-of-hadrian-and-reflections-on-the-composition-of-memoirs-of-hadr">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=french">french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=top-10-2013">top-10-2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer177466259" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2403761274325388808">In 2009, Hugo Chavez, in an impromptu meeting with Barack Obama, handed the newly-elected American President a copy of <i>The Open Veins of Latin America</i> by Eduardo Galeano. Chavez wanted Obama to learn from Literature of the exploitation of Latin America. He had hope the young President would be open-minded, and a reader. Obama’s advisers quickly and glibly disabused the hopeful by saying the book was in Spanish, a language the President didn’t know. <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.theguardian.com/world/2009/apr/19/obama-chavez-book-gift-latin-america'>http://www.theguardian.com/world/2009...</a><br/><br/>It’s do</span>
  <span id="freeText2403761274325388808" style="display:none">In 2009, Hugo Chavez, in an impromptu meeting with Barack Obama, handed the newly-elected American President a copy of <i>The Open Veins of Latin America</i> by Eduardo Galeano. Chavez wanted Obama to learn from Literature of the exploitation of Latin America. He had hope the young President would be open-minded, and a reader. Obama’s advisers quickly and glibly disabused the hopeful by saying the book was in Spanish, a language the President didn’t know. <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.theguardian.com/world/2009/apr/19/obama-chavez-book-gift-latin-america">http://www.theguardian.com/world/2009...</a><br /><br />It’s doubtful President Obama will be inviting me any time soon for that afternoon beer, and, if he did, I would not be so presumptuous as to press a book in his hands. But, you know, if he asked, I would make that pained face like I do, pause dramatically, and with a grave “Perhaps it’s not too late” offer him <i>The Memoirs of Hadrian</i>. For, after all, it doesn’t matter if our leaders are young or old, black or white, male or female; it doesn’t matter if they like a little oral in the oval or can’t pronounce ‘nuclear’. I just want them to be wise. It may be too late for that in this day and age of handlers and YouTube and issue-driven voters. But think: Hadrian followed Caligula, Claudius and Nero.<br /><br />Why read, and for that matter, why write a book about a long-ago Emperor? In her <i>Reflections</i> following this novel, Marguerite Yourcenar explains, “I fell to making, and then re-making, this portrait of a man who was <i>almost</i> wise.” (emphasis in original).<br /><br /><i>Almost</i>.<br /><br />The lessons are anecdotal. Returning the daughter of an Asian ruler, captured in her infancy, without haggling, serves a better end than sending in the legions. Absorb the knowledge of a colony instead of its tribute. Keep your friends close and your enemies closer. Even: let execution suffice when execution after torture seems, you know, excessive. If you two can’t play nice, well, I’ll build a wall.<br /><br />He watched his subjects come to touch his hand, believing his ‘divinity’ would be a cure of disease or illness; and he gave them hope, and sometimes cure, yet knowing that his own body could not be saved.<br /><br />This is a Novel in Memoir form. That’s hard to imagine, I suppose. But there are epistolary novels and novels in diary form, so why not this? It’s better than History. <i>Historians propose to us systems too perfect for explaining the past, with sequence of cause and effect much too exact and clear to have been ever entirely true.</i> Still, it doesn’t <i>feel</i> like a novel. That’s not a criticism. It’s just that the scholarship is, frankly, breathtaking. It is also respectful. The language, though, the language is lush.<br /><br /><i>The written word has taught me to listen to the human voice, much as the great unchanging statues have taught me to appreciate bodily motions.</i><br /><br /><i>Although a weaver would wish to mend his web or a clever calculator would correct his mistakes, and the artist would try to retouch his masterpiece if still imperfect or slightly damaged. Nature prefers to start again from the very clay, from chaos itself, and this horrible waste is what we term natural order.</i><br /><br />This is also a love story, though certainly not for Hadrian and his much younger, ‘arranged’ wife. No, Hadrian was in love with Antinous, a lovely teenage boy. Yourcenar spares us the sex but not the obsession. This time Divinity did not cure, it killed. And so the <i>Memoir</i> is tinged with regret, for being <i>almost</i> wise.<br /><br />I found myself pausing often at Yourcenar’s aphoristic tone. I wrote many lessons down. I also found my mind wandering often through the obligatory historical chronology. But after I turned the final page, the importance of this started to play with the light. Oh how I wish our leaders were wise. Or <i>almost</i> wise.<br /><br />----- ----- ----- -----<br /><br />I almost didn’t write this review because I read Kelly’s, which is one of the Great reviews, here or elsewhere -- <i>Memoirs of Hadrian is a meditation of finding a pile of pottery shards and deciding what to do with them</i>. And I read Aubrey’s <i>Ode</i> to this book, itself a work of art. But this isn’t a competition. Right, Hadrian?<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2403761274325388808" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1637594085" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1637594085" />
    <a title="Raul Bimenyimana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43664238-raul-bimenyimana"><img alt="Raul Bimenyimana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1466510542p2/43664238.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1637594085?book_show_action=true">Feb 22, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Raul Bimenyimana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Raul Bimenyimana" href="/user/show/43664238-raul-bimenyimana">Raul Bimenyimana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/2496418.Memoirs_of_Hadrian">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43664238-raul-bimenyimana?shelf=women-writers">women-writers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43664238-raul-bimenyimana?shelf=queer">queer</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43664238-raul-bimenyimana?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1637594085" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13899870144236906857">Written in epistolary form, Hadrian writes to his adopted grandson Marcus Aurelius. This book begins as an update after Hadrian sees his physician Hermogenes and later develops into the reflection and recollection of his life. This book is beautiful, from beginning to the end, beautiful.<br/><br/>From his childhood in Spain, to his army life, to the beginnings of his political life, his ascent to power and career successes, his policies as well as love for art and Greek culture, his finding his love Antin</span>
  <span id="freeText13899870144236906857" style="display:none">Written in epistolary form, Hadrian writes to his adopted grandson Marcus Aurelius. This book begins as an update after Hadrian sees his physician Hermogenes and later develops into the reflection and recollection of his life. This book is beautiful, from beginning to the end, beautiful.<br /><br />From his childhood in Spain, to his army life, to the beginnings of his political life, his ascent to power and career successes, his policies as well as love for art and Greek culture, his finding his love Antinous, loss and the grief he suffers, and later old age and approaching death. This was a wonderful, wonderful reading experience.<br /><br />Of course Hadrian has always been considered a great statesman and was indeed an impressive figure. Some of his policies were meant to help improve the conditions of slaves, women and other marginalized groups in Roman society; he also relatively maintains peace and avoids war, especially compared to his previous predecessor, in the empire. However, he is still determined to maintain the status quo and systems that made his rise to power possible, and keeping the empire and colonies intact, even if it means a war that murdered hundreds of thousands and the destruction it led to as was the case with Jerusalem.<br /><br />Reading the book, it was the writer Marguerite Yourcenar and not Hadrian that was at the back of my mind with every read sentence. She worked to bridge the distance of eighteen centuries that separated her from Hadrian, reconstructing that world that is long vanished save for the histories, ruins and artworks from the period and worked successfully to give a self-portrait of the emperor. <i>She</i> was the one more impressive to me and not the emperor, even though she claims her role was as a sorcerer:<br /><br /><i>"The sorcerer who pricks his thumb before he evokes the shades knows well that they will heed his call only because they lap his blood. He knows, too, or ought to know, that the voices who speak to him are wiser and more worthy of attention than are his own clamorous outcries."</i><br /><br />This is a project that was a three decade process; reading the writer's reflection on composition, which was part of the library copy I had, was a fascinating reveal into the process that bore this book as well as the research the writer undertook and the bibliography notes provided. How she was able to dissolve history, facts, hypotheses and invention and have it all merge, formed and crystallized into the marvel that is this book, how she did it all, I'm still impressed. This is a remarkable artistic achievement.<br /><br />The photographs illustrated and selected by the writer, along with the reflections on composition and bibliography notes contribute to the wonderful reading experience. The glowing and brilliant reviews the book has received are more than merited.<br /><br />The English translation of the book was also impressive. Reading this at times I forgot that it was a translation, translated from French by Yourcenar's partner Grace Fick who worked in collaboration with the writer and who the writer also acknowledges for her role in the writing of the book (not related to the translating). This was brilliant, it was with a mixture of awe, admiration and envy that I read this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13899870144236906857" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2137569747" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2137569747" />
    <a title="Emilio Berra" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/69897903-emilio-berra"><img alt="Emilio Berra" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502015157p2/69897903.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2137569747?book_show_action=true">Sep 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emilio Berra" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emilio Berra" href="/user/show/69897903-emilio-berra">Emilio Berra</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788806174088" href="/book/show/2826538-memorie-di-adriano-seguite-dai-taccuini-di-appunti">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/69897903-emilio-berra?shelf=letto">letto</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2137569747" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10487760478629994892"> Adriano, uomo e imperatore<br/>Un libro bellissimo di Marguerite Yourcenar, scrittrice meravigliosa.<br/><br/>Io-narrante è l'imperatore Adriano, ormai sessantenne e malato, che scorre la propria vita alla luce di riflessioni a carattere esistenziale.<br/>Chi conosce l'autrice può scrgervi, però, molte convergenze di pensiero. Anche lei sapeva come la gloria e gli averi non siano che vile moneta, da far dire al protagonista : "Tornai a casa coperto di onori, ma ero invecchiato" , e pone in risalto la figura di Plo</span>
  <span id="freeText10487760478629994892" style="display:none"> Adriano, uomo e imperatore<br />Un libro bellissimo di Marguerite Yourcenar, scrittrice meravigliosa.<br /><br />Io-narrante è l'imperatore Adriano, ormai sessantenne e malato, che scorre la propria vita alla luce di riflessioni a carattere esistenziale.<br />Chi conosce l'autrice può scrgervi, però, molte convergenze di pensiero. Anche lei sapeva come la gloria e gli averi non siano che vile moneta, da far dire al protagonista : "Tornai a casa coperto di onori, ma ero invecchiato" , e pone in risalto la figura di Plotina, vedova del predecessore Traiano, che " si dedicava ai piaceri austeri della meditazione e dei libri. (...) Scendeva soavemente nell'ombra ; quel giardino, quelle stanze chiare diventavano ogni giorno di più il recinto di una Musa" . Così diversa da molte donne di quell'epoca di decadenza, per certi aspetti tanto simili alle signore omologate di oggi e di ieri ; modelli femminili da cui la nostra scrittrice volle radicalmente emanciparsi : " l'amore di cui parlavano continuamente, a volte mi sembrava fatuo (...) un gioiello alla moda, un accessorio costoso e fragile ; e sospettavo che si dessero la passione insieme al rossetto (...). Avrei desiderato molto di più la creatura umana spoglia, sola con se stessa". "Un uomo che legge, e che pensa (...) appartiene alla specie, non al sesso (...) . Ma le amanti pareva si facessero una gloria di non pensare se non da donne; lo spirito, l'anima, che cercavo non era anch'essa che un profumo" . "Ritrovavo la visuale limitata delle donne, (...) il loro cielo grigio non appena cessa di ridervi l'amore" . <br /><br />La narrazione, pur sorretta da una documentatissima base storica, ha un taglio prettamente esistenziale.<br />Lo stile possiede lo splendore di una scrittura capace di sorprendere sempre, e sovente l'intelligenza vi brilla come un diamante.<br />Splendida la traduzione della latinista Lidia Storoni Mazzolani.<br /><br />"Piccola anima smarrita e soave, compagna e ospite del corpo, ora t'appresti a scendere in luoghi incolori (...) . Un istante ancora, guardiamo insieme le rive familiari, le cose che certamente non vedremo mai più... Cerchiamo d'entrare nella morte a occhi aperti" .</span>
  <a data-text-id="10487760478629994892" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_107217" class="review notext" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/107217" />
    <a title="Amanda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7667-amanda"><img alt="Amanda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1375147883p2/7667.jpg" /></a>

<div id="review_1676944" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1676944" />
    <a title="Siria" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/115473-siria"><img alt="Siria" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1332731316p2/115473.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1676944?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Siria" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Siria" href="/user/show/115473-siria">Siria</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/115473-siria?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/115473-siria?shelf=french-fiction">french-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/115473-siria?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/115473-siria?shelf=queer-lit">queer-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1676944" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3882202075868896768">I adored this book, really and truly. Yourcenar’s prose, even in translation, is a triumph. The novel takes the form of a letter written by Hadrian to his heir presumptive, the young Marcus Aurelius; and in it, she really manages to capture some of the stately elegance of which Latin is capable, even though she was writing in another language. Her prose flows beautifully; it’s such a joy to read.<br/><br/>For a biographical novel concerned with the life – and the leaving of it – by a Roman emperor, she ne</span>
  <span id="freeText3882202075868896768" style="display:none">I adored this book, really and truly. Yourcenar’s prose, even in translation, is a triumph. The novel takes the form of a letter written by Hadrian to his heir presumptive, the young Marcus Aurelius; and in it, she really manages to capture some of the stately elegance of which Latin is capable, even though she was writing in another language. Her prose flows beautifully; it’s such a joy to read.<br /><br />For a biographical novel concerned with the life – and the leaving of it – by a Roman emperor, she never really goes into too much detail on the facts of his reign, or the people by whom he was surrounded. Yourcenar is much more concerned with how Hadrian sees the world around him, his philosophy of life, and the function which he sees the empire as having. For all that some of his actions would seem reprehensible, and some of his ideas mistaken, to a modern reader, I always found myself sympathising with him because Yourcenar conveyed his world view so well. There is a very real sense of inhabiting the (mental) world of ancient Rome for a time when reading this.<br /><br />Such a brilliant psychological study, and a very persuasive and plausible recreation of what Hadrian might have been like as a man. Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3882202075868896768" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17082908904770815515">This is an unlikely candidate to be on my all-time list of the 5 best books I've ever read. But it is. An "autobiographical" account of a long-dead Roman emperor (not even one of the glamorous, or truly filthy ones), written by a 20th century Frenchwoman - who'd have thunk it?<br/><br/>And yet, I beg, I implore you - if you haven't already - buy, steal, or scrounge a copy of this book.   <span id="freeText17082908904770815515" style="display:none">This is an unlikely candidate to be on my all-time list of the 5 best books I've ever read. But it is. An "autobiographical" account of a long-dead Roman emperor (not even one of the glamorous, or truly filthy ones), written by a 20th century Frenchwoman - who'd have thunk it?<br /><br />And yet, I beg, I implore you - if you haven't already - buy, steal, or scrounge a copy of this book. It will draw you in. Can't promise it will change your life. All I know is that I find myself re-reading it every two or three years. Why? Because each time I learn something more about what it is to be human. A <b> sodden </b> cliché, but I don't know how else to put it.</span>

<div id="review_3323493" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3323493" />
    <a title="Nathan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/174400-nathan"><img alt="Nathan" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3323493?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nathan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nathan" href="/user/show/174400-nathan">Nathan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3323493" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6186799666149088640">With the subject matter, this book could have been dry and dull, but in Marguerite Yourcenar's hands this pretend batch of letters from Hadrian to his young successor, Marcus Aurelius, is a fluid, touching, and disturbing meditation on historical forces and processes, as well as an incredible character study based on historical record. She was the first woman to be inducted into the French Acadamy, and the New Yorker made the claim a while back that she displayed the highest level of erudition o</span>
  <span id="freeText6186799666149088640" style="display:none">With the subject matter, this book could have been dry and dull, but in Marguerite Yourcenar's hands this pretend batch of letters from Hadrian to his young successor, Marcus Aurelius, is a fluid, touching, and disturbing meditation on historical forces and processes, as well as an incredible character study based on historical record. She was the first woman to be inducted into the French Acadamy, and the New Yorker made the claim a while back that she displayed the highest level of erudition of any author in any language in the 20th century, pointedly noting that the company she beats out includes Joyce. Its a bold statement, and not a bad guess. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6186799666149088640" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('1oQMcMpk9yR2WqgbXW2uKmlC1oW46WV09bUpNvq7x7rsomWicTo+TNFxHHqqmKT1vx0xTXZk6xOuy2+HN/g1IA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('4HUo2Z1dqXrWztPTGmYx2oJlpVaPcjJkc91nBYjRRonaU0ELJgNgEnHlZ7LtkzsFVDpCnkH/vAMooyG0RZK0Ew==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Called in to investigate, melancholy Detective Ciccio, a secret admirer of the murdered woman and a friend of her husband’s, discovers that almost everyone in the apartment building is somehow involved in the case, and with each new development the mystery only deepens and broadens. Gadda’s sublimely different detective story presents a scathing picture of fascist Italy while tracking the elusiveness of the truth, the impossibility of proof, and the infinite complexity of the workings of fate, showing how they come into conflict with the demands of justice and love. Italo Calvino, Pier Paolo Pasolini, and Alberto Moravia all considered That Awful Mess on the Via Merulana to be the great modern Italian novel. It is a deceptively simple story – five characters, the events of a few days, the intrigues of families and lovers.\n\nThe place is Rome. The central figure is Michele, a young man in confused but furious rebellion against the emptiness of bourgeois life. His father is dead; his mother, Mariagrazia, desperately clings to her bored lover, Leo; his sister has no hope of marriage or career and bleakly prepares to give herself to Leo as well. A frequent visitor is Leo\'s former lover, Lisa, ostensibly Mariagrazia\'s friend, a woman who feels she is in the final late bloom before age destroys beauty. She longs to make Michele her lover, but he is bored and disgusted by her pretenses, her vanity, her desperation.\n\nAll five are cast loose on the sea of modern life – obsessed with what they want, what they feel they are owed, the wrongs that have been done them, their loneliness.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/7732.Marguerite_Yourcenar">About Marguerite Yourcenar</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/7732.Marguerite_Yourcenar"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1483207978p3/7732.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/7732.Marguerite_Yourcenar">Marguerite Yourcenar
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13988359741059738616">The first woman to be elected to The French Academy, the multi talented personality of Marguerite Yourcenar was a novelist, essayist, playwright, short story writer, poet and translator. She was an artist at reconstructing historical eras in the form of her novels. Her novels, dealing with modern issues set in historical eras won her immense fame as a writer.<br/><br/>The inheritance Yourcenar received afte</span>
  <span id="freeText13988359741059738616" style="display:none">The first woman to be elected to The French Academy, the multi talented personality of Marguerite Yourcenar was a novelist, essayist, playwright, short story writer, poet and translator. She was an artist at reconstructing historical eras in the form of her novels. Her novels, dealing with modern issues set in historical eras won her immense fame as a writer.<br /><br />The inheritance Yourcenar received after the death of her father, allowed her to devote herself to traveling and literary pursuits and love affairs on the Paris artistic scene. She joined a bohemian crowd of artists, moving between Paris, Lausanne, Athens, Istanbul, Brussels and the Greek islands, which inspired her travel writing "Nouvelles Orientales" and "Feux" based on Greek mythology.<br /><br />Yourcenar was among the first female writers to live an openly lesbian lifestyle, and she moved to the USA in 1939 to live with her partner Grace Frick. The couple were to remain together until Frick’s death in 1979. She taught French History and History of Art, and continued to write: her novel "Mémoires d’Hadrien" was published in 1951 and enjoyed success with both the critics and the public worldwide. <br /><br />She was an inveterate traveller and remained active right up to her death in 1987. She was a militant vegetarian and defender of the rights of animals and inspired Brigitte Bardot to set up her animal sanctuary.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13988359741059738616" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
